I hate to ask, but from my own research it seems this cruise came nowhere near the actual geographic north pole. According to their website, the trip takes passengers 700km away from land at a 90 degree angle towards North, and yet the distance between Longyearbyen and the geographic north pole is approx 1300km, or nearly double the 700km journey. So what's going on, why would you pay 30,000 euro for going to the north pole when you're not actually going to the north pole?

In case you havenâ€™t figured it out yet, geographic North Pole is the physical point on earth at North 90 degrees; Magnetic North is an imaginary point. The earthâ€™s magnetic pole shifts every so often and so magnetic pole changes itâ€™s location as well. Since magnetic north (and therefore also magnetic south) is the only thing we could measure reasonably, navigators uses magnetic north as the reference, and depending on where in the world they are at, correct for the magnetic declination (variation), which will derive to geographic north ğŸ˜ hope this make some sense

Hey! I teach Navigation - among other things - and here is the difference between magnetic North Pole and geographic North Pole! The geographic North Pole is just that. The very top of the earth. In latitude and longitude it is 90 degrees north latitude. The geographic center of the northern hemisphere. Longitude lines all meet at the Geographic North Pole (and at the geographic South Pole) The Magnetic North Pole is where compasses point, (it is currently located at 86.50Â°N and 164.04Â°E ) which is off set (and always moving a little) from the geographic North Pole. So the earth, is actually a big ball with an iron core, like a bar magnet. That bar magnet is floating in liquid rock. A compass points to the north end of the bar magnet. The difference between magnetic north and geographic north is called declination or magnetic variation. And we need to account for it when doing navigation.

I just can't let it slide im sorry! ğŸ˜‚  Dijon Mustard used to be grown and processed in all of France, but in the early nineteen-hundreds, the Frenchmen discovered a new type of mustard seed. The Seed they were growing at the time was the commonly used white mustard seed. But the seed they discovered in the town of Dijon where a black one, thereby the name of Dijon. It proved hard to make the black seeded plant grow tho, and therefore black seeded farms slowly shifted to a new country with better conditions for growing them.  CAN you guess the country?  Hint: the First word of my last sentence. Yes! correct, it's Canada. For some weird reason, black seeds grow better in cold environments and are therefore normally grown in Canada.  It is still considered true Dijon if the mustard in the glass contains more than 25% of locally grown seeds from Dijon and is packaged in Dijon France.            -Your dose of completely random unnecessary facts.
